  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_654ef3f6-af6b-4019-ab3b-8a7ba728f69c'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<span id="freeTextContainer9209490010407970712"><strong>The Thrilling Sequel To Sailing To Sarantium</strong><br/><br/>Beckoned by the Emperor Valerius, Crispin, a renowned mosaicist, has arrived in the fabled city of Sarantium. Here he seeks to fulfill his artistic ambitions and his destiny high upon a dome that will become the emerror's magnificent sanctuary and legacy.<br/><br/>But the beauty and solitude of his work cannot protect his from Sarantium's </span>
  <span id="freeText9209490010407970712" style="display:none"><strong>The Thrilling Sequel To Sailing To Sarantium</strong><br /><br />Beckoned by the Emperor Valerius, Crispin, a renowned mosaicist, has arrived in the fabled city of Sarantium. Here he seeks to fulfill his artistic ambitions and his destiny high upon a dome that will become the emerror's magnificent sanctuary and legacy.<br /><br />But the beauty and solitude of his work cannot protect his from Sarantium's intrigue. Beneath him the city swirls with rumors of war and conspiracy, while otherworldly fires mysteriously flicker and disappear in the streets at night. Valerius is looking west to Crispin's homeland to reunite an Empire -- a plan that may have dire consequences for the loved ones Crispin left behind.<br /><br />In Sarantium, however, loyalty is always complex, for Crispin's fate has become entwined with that of Valerius and his Empress, as well as Queen Gisel, his own monarch exiled in Sarantium herself. And now another voyager -- this time from the east -- has arrived, a pysician determined to make his mark amid the shifting, treachearous currents of passion and violence that will determine the empire's fate.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9209490010407970712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1872350063" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1872350063" />
    <a title="Choko" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27962805-choko"><img alt="Choko" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475642755p2/27962805.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1872350063?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Choko" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Choko" href="/user/show/27962805-choko">Choko</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition 9780007352074" href="/book/show/19276892-lord-of-emperors">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27962805-choko?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27962805-choko?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1872350063" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6324607598057323156"><b>*** 5 ***<br/><br/>A buddy read with the Kay Squad @ FBR, because sometimes we need something to feed the soul!!!</b><br/><br/>I am not going to beat around the bush. I have been melancholy. Many reasons, mostly Real Life is a B@#$h, and I have been struggling with reading anything. However, you know that moment while you are reading something and your soul gets heavier at first, then starts pulsing with life again and you realize you are reading something remarkable? This is what happened toward the end of this duolog</span>
  <span id="freeText6324607598057323156" style="display:none"><b>*** 5 ***<br /><br />A buddy read with the Kay Squad @ FBR, because sometimes we need something to feed the soul!!!</b><br /><br />I am not going to beat around the bush. I have been melancholy. Many reasons, mostly Real Life is a B@#$h, and I have been struggling with reading anything. However, you know that moment while you are reading something and your soul gets heavier at first, then starts pulsing with life again and you realize you are reading something remarkable? This is what happened toward the end of this duology. I wanted to cry, but I also felt awakened to life and was reminded of all the beauty and wonder that surrounds us, if only we allow ourselves to see it. Yes, there is plenty of ugly and hard and horrid around us as well, but it only gives us the opportunity to appreciate the miracle and Majesty of the "thing", the power, the omnipresent "something" which gives breath to us all, gives color, form and sound to nature, and gives us the ability to perceive it! <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"...“The great events of an age appear, to those living through them, as backdrops only to the vastly more compelling dramas of their own lives, and how could it be otherwise?<br />In this same way, many of the men and women there in the Hippodrome (and some who were not, but later claimed to have been) would cling to one private image or another of what transpired. They might be entirely different things, varying moments, for each of us has strings within the soul, and we are played upon in different ways, like instruments, and how could it be otherwise?”..."</i>
</b><br /><br />Growing up I had many interests, many loves and quite a few passions. Among those were music, dance, theater and art. Even better if I could have them in any combination - a glutton of beauty and sensory input. I often ditched school to go to a play, an art exhibition or the Circus, which was my private self-indulgence. I was like a drug addict, only my drug was the way human artists, each in their medium, funneled their artistry down my gullet, never enough and always amazing! I had learned how to appreciate not only the works which showed greatness or the promise of it, but also those works which struggled to be more than they were, the ones which talent had passed by and only ambition kept together, those which had scarcely anything to do with art, but showed parts of the makeup of their creators nonetheless. Every line, note, tone, syllable, hue, shape, or resonance, each symbol a key to another mystery. Needless to say, I got kicked out of school and had to finish as a private student, while as much as I personally tried to enter into the teachings of all those beloved artistic disciplines, I was never good enough in my own eyes to get anything but frustration to go with my understanding of the divinity in the spark of all natural talents. <br /><br />Relying on his own vast and supple talent, Guy Gavriel Kay tells us a story of political intrigue at the second century of greatness in a city and world based on Byzantium in the 5th or 6th centuries. Sarantium, a city of power, the seat of the Emperor of the East, the place where the contemporary greats go to unleash their talents, be it as chariot racers, as soldiers, as politicians, as dancers, actors, prostitutes, doctors or artisans. This is where Crispin, our main protagonist and very gifted mosaicist, is working on the dome of the biggest hose of worship ever created. It is the crowning jewel of the rain of Emperor Valentine II, his legacy, since he and his beloved wife, the exquisite ex-dancer Aliana had no children or other hairs... The two of them are overwhelmingly smart and savvy in court intrigue, elevating it to art of machinations. Crispin, with his obvious likability and openness of manner wins them and many other influential people over, getting himself tangled up in their games of power. No matter how many times he made the point that he is only an "artisan", the red-headed mosaicist was instrumental in the faith of the emerging new powers in the region. <br /><br />With the winds of Fate blowing in from different directions, the job Crispin is charged with changes and loses its initial purpose, to which Crispin was so devoted. With riots and wars waiting in the not-so-far-away-distance, priorities change and the value of beauty always seem to diminish when survival becomes a primary concern. GGK is brilliant in the way he parcels the story in perfect bites, creating a mosaic of words, each small piece important to the whole, but each insignificant compared to the overall image. He gives us the little pieces, we know them, develop attachments to them, learn of their vulnerabilities and strengths through actions and situations, not through info-dumps or bogging down by endless angst or over-description. Each character has its destiny, its prominence in the sea of others just by being unique, having its small role to play with its adventures or daily grind. And if any one of them was in a different place at a different time, moved one this way than that, made a slightly divergent choice, the end result would be completely altered. There are no useless pieces in this artistic creation. <br /><br /><b>
  <i>"...“The room seemed laden and layered with intricacies of past and present and what was to come. Nuances coiling and spreading like incense, subtle and insistent.”..."</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>This author speaks to me, he reaches into my soul and pulls on my heartstrings with virtuosity I have rarely encountered. </b> But just as all art, it speaks to all of us in various ways and I cannot say that he is for everyone. I know some of my friends did not enjoy this book as much as I, but this is the beauty of Life - our diversity not only in form, but in mind and soul. The conclusion of this series was breathtakingly moving for me for so many reasons, I would have to write a tome just to count the ways:) But the main way the author triumphs is in the ability to show us how to see and feel the beauty of artistic creativity and the innate human talents. He venerates the things which makes us profoundly singular in the world we live in. ... Once again, I warned you I have been melancholy:). This book did not make me happy, it just gave me back the wonder of wanting to keep on living and loving and appreciating all that we are given in this to fragile, to fleeting, but utterly precious Life!!! Thank you, GGK!<br /><br />I wish you all Happy Reading and may you always find what you Need in the pages of a Good Book!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="6324607598057323156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6400351820339405424" style="display:none">I was thinking for a long time what to write about this book. I let my emotions cool down a bit before I write anything because "OH MY GOOD THIS IS FUCKING GREAT" type of review seems out of character. After some consideration I decided that I mostly said everything in in my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2130915629?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">review of the first book </a> but there is one big difference.<br /><br />In that review I wrote:<br /><i>Story is slow paced and not that eventful and without huge twists or tear jerking moments.</i><br />This doesn't apply here. Here we see story develop much faster as some big events unfold and we see some very emotional moments. We see subtle writing from first book describe some very unsubtle times.<br /><br />In conclusion I hesitate to call this one of my favorite fantasy books simply because of how little it plays out like fantasy.In any hypothetical conversation about this series I'm dropping "fantasy" but "favorite" stays.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2130408942" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2130408942" />
    <a title="Mayim de Vries" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50424456-mayim-de-vries"><img alt="Mayim de Vries" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560508357p2/50424456.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2130408942?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mayim de Vries" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mayim de Vries" href="/user/show/50424456-mayim-de-vries">Mayim de Vries</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2130408942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15608364350112990175">So, how does one make a mosaic? <br/><br/>First, you mix quicklime for the setting bed. Then, you tend to it in the oven making sure the texture is just right. Once this has been accomplished, you lay it smoothly on the surface. Only then can you start placing the tesserae in the setting bed.<br/><br/><i>tes·ser·a (tĕs′ər-ə) n. pl. tes·ser·ae (tĕs′ə-rē′). One of the small squares of stone or glass used in making mosaic patterns.</i><br/><br/>The previous book in the duology accounts for first three steps (under the disguise of talk</span>
  <span id="freeText15608364350112990175" style="display:none">So, how does one make a mosaic? <br /><br />First, you mix quicklime for the setting bed. Then, you tend to it in the oven making sure the texture is just right. Once this has been accomplished, you lay it smoothly on the surface. Only then can you start placing the tesserae in the setting bed.<br /><br /><i>tes·ser·a (tĕs′ər-ə) n. pl. tes·ser·ae (tĕs′ə-rē′). One of the small squares of stone or glass used in making mosaic patterns.</i><br /><br />The previous book in the duology accounts for first three steps (under the disguise of talking about life changing experiences). My main problem back then was the fact that I couldn’t quite put my finger on what is the main axis, the guiding motif for Kay. Only now do I understand fully, that he was just setting his ground, assembling and preparing the gems of individual characters for the great design of this two-volume masterpiece. <b>Previously we were given a sketch, a contour of the creation, in the Lord of Emperors, the space is filled with light and darkness and all the hues in between.</b><br /><br /><b>This book is a design equal to the vastness of the setting: Sarantium (basically Byzantium</b> in the apex of its glory, a little before Justinian opened St. Sophia and a little after closed the Academy of Plato) ruled by a pair of formidable monarchs surrounded by the court full of dangerous people where nothing can be taken for what it seemed to be and where nuanced intrigues are coiling and spreading like incense, subtle and insistent. At the same time a city of cities inhabited by thousands of people charioteers and cooks, dancers and soldiers, clerks and doctors, all with their own dreams and pathways, plots and counterplots separate and yet intertwined under the very same sun. <br /><br /><i>“The great events of an age appear, to those living through them, as backdrops only to the vastly more compelling dramas of their own lives, and how could it be otherwise?… The central moments of an age occur on the margins of the lives of most people.”</i><br /><br />Our main protagonist, <b>Caius Crispus, sailed to Sarantium commissioned to create a mosaic</b> in the new Sanctuary built by the Emperor. A man who ruled gazing on generations yet to be born as much as on those he ruled already, a man of visions as breathtaking as they were dangerous. And yet, Sarantium is not a place in which one found refuge, even in pursuit of a vision and so <b>Crispin is caught in the turning gyres of events unfolding. There is the barren ruling pair searching for legacy, military and ecclesiastic interests on the crossroads between strategy and theology, revenges to be taken, loves to be stolen, races to be won, dances to be danced, and deaths to be administered, and avoided. Lives to be done and undone, sometimes spent and sometimes discarded.</b> <br /><br /><b>I will be very frank: Kay is a genius. I know whole books containing less plot, emotion, and beauty than his single chapter.</b> In his novels everything is coming together so effortlessly that in a book of a lesser writer it would scream "fake" like a topiary in a middle of a French garden. Here, it is masterful, with each detail fitting all other details but in a natural way, it is like a contained force of nature, simply an art. <br /><br />The way Kay <b>places the events both in space and time is unparalleled</b> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ for instance the way he manages to weave so much into a single night in the last chapters of book I. I almost felt like all the people walking around Sarantium to and from lovers' beds were missing each other by couple of steps. It was a very cinematic sequence with the ominous litter of the fat perv in the background. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Those thirsty for action, will have the whole Hippodrome drama and chariot races to quicken their pulse. Furthermore, <b>not a single detail is wasted</b>. For example, at one point in the book he gives us an image of a person with no solitude; it is very beautiful (if frightening) but seemingly trivial. And yet, this detail proves to be crucial in the grand scheme of things. There are <b>patterns within patterns, changing depending on perspective and light, just like a real mosaic does. Lord of the Emperors is a rare gem, but in order to appreciate it fully, I think you need to be a mature, attentive reader.</b> <br /><br />Initially I thought that the title refers to some holy divinity, regardless of the name. In reality, <b>the novel talks about time that rules us all</b>, grinds us down equally without any preference to race, sex, religion, profession or station in life. Or maybe, even more accurately, <b>about the evanescence of all things. About our legacies.</b> Kay asks us to ponder about what do we want to leave behind and how would we like to be remembered? What truly matters in the end?<br /><br />Because time claims us all, it is the chroniclers, the painters, sculptors, the historians, the people who filter us into immortality through art and craft who truly reign. Maybe this is the reason why an artisan is the main protagonist. Maybe this is also the reason why we are warned about the fragility of beauty and the defining impermanence of art that actually only intensifies its glory. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I thought the way Crispin's work has been undone was fiendishly clever. It has all been out there in the light from the beginning, waiting for a smart reader (not me) too see it. And the references to iconoclasm! The historian in me wept with delight. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It is only fitting that a book about art and beauty is written in an exquisitely artful and beautiful way. Soul-wrenching really <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the death of the Emperor wrecked me emotionally. It has been superbly written, but I simply couldn't do more than a couple of pages at one go <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br /><i>“It was a truth of the world that certain women carried a magic about them and dreams followed where they went.”</i><br /><br />Just as it has been in the previous instalment, <b>Sarantium is a city of women, for it is the women, who shape the story</b>, not the men with their armies and blades. They are beautiful, smart, and deadly - certainly not pawns in someone else's games.<br /><br />There are also <b>villains to abhor and to admire</b> (ice with an edge of malice), oh so beautiful in their personal tragedies. Caught in their own passions and enslaved by their own pasts they plan disasters as effortlessly as we prepare shopping lists, both unwilling and unable to change the (pre)destined course. I love how Kay gives us a glimpse of what could have been a happily ever after and with the other hand an understanding that this future could never come to pass. He plays with the <b>layered paradoxes and double-edged ironies of fate, and everything coming around in circles</b>. <br /><br /><i>“I am what I've been made to be. Don't be deceived.” </i><br /><br />There were things I didn’t like that much. Some protagonists, crucial in the previous book, fade into the background, much to my chagrin <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and if you liked Kasia, you will be equally saddened for she is moved to the margins <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The sexual chemistry between Crispin and several women is worrying - the man must be sex on legs! *I exclaim with a touch of exasperation* because he is entangled in intimacies and not each occasion these had been an act of contrivance convenience or intrigue. I don't like the epilogue, I must confess <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Especially, the Empress' fate bothers me. After all, this is the very same woman who preferred to die Empress than sail away to safety during the Victory Riot. This just doesn't fit with the woman who found Crispin in Varena and "go in living" still wearing the imperial scent! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but I cannot deny the perfection of design. This masterpiece can be read in many ways <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ as Andrew of the Kay squad pointed out, the fact that she still wears her own scent could equally be a subtle suggestion that Aliana still has the strength of an Empress, regardless of the changes she's endured  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <b>There is a happy ending without a happy ending. Villains are defeated but you mourn for them. Those who win do so with bitter tang in the victory.</b> <br /><br />When I finished the book I felt “an emptiness invade me like an army”, an abyss burdened with images. What is the reader when the book is done? What is the reader to do? <br /><br />Read another Kay, that is the only answer I have. <br /><br />----<br />My review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2112183584?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Sailing to Sarantium</a><br /><br />This was a buddy read with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/18601016-sailing-to-sarantium-september-2017" rel="nofollow noopener">Kay Squad</a> at FBR.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15608364350112990175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6572378470882263528">Lyrical, powerful and engaging since the beginning. The second part of this duet raises the stakes of this “game of countries” even higher, and the way the story's tapestry is woven is as exquisite as it could be expected, if not more.<br/>Against the backdrop of "the city of performers, the heart of things", which has attracted so many people with different goals and desires, the characters meet, mingle, clash or form alliances, and layer after layer of deceptions, ambitions and dreams peels off wit</span>
  <span id="freeText6572378470882263528" style="display:none">Lyrical, powerful and engaging since the beginning. The second part of this duet raises the stakes of this “game of countries” even higher, and the way the story's tapestry is woven is as exquisite as it could be expected, if not more.<br />Against the backdrop of "the city of performers, the heart of things", which has attracted so many people with different goals and desires, the characters meet, mingle, clash or form alliances, and layer after layer of deceptions, ambitions and dreams peels off with impeccable timing and pace.<br /><br /><i>"Nuances coiling and spreading like incense, subtle and insistent."</i><br /><br />I loved the complex plot and the high drama; I only have a minor gripe regarding a certain event toward the end, not in itself (very satisfying) but rather with how it comes about. That aside, I ended up feeling like I was sailing to Sarantium myself so engrossed I was in the tale and its compelling characters. I also experienced the melancholy, hope and beauty that always pervades Kay's stories and that, at the same time, it’s tailored and unique for each of them.<br /><br />A wondrous journey.<br /><br /><i>Just as it is adversity that hardens the spirit of a people, so can adversity strengthen the soul of a man. What we master becomes ours to use.</i></span>

  <span id="freeText11555870494085490949" style="display:none">9/10<br /><br />Lord of Emperors is another great example of Kay’s talent as in this book he has added much more characters and making a more complex story than the previous one, full with intrigues, machinations, betrayals and some great twists.<br /><br />The story continues with Rustem, a physician from Bassania, as he is summoned by the King of Kings to save his life but to take also a mission to Sarantium that may well bring him to great danger and will, probably, may cost him his own life in the way. However, along the way he’ll find himself in many dilemmas as he’ll have to face, not only the intrigues of Sarantium, but also and his own beliefs.<br />In the meantime, Crispin, having begun his dream of decorating the Sanctuary of Jad he’ll find himself in difficult situations as he will have choose between fiends and enemies, love and betrayal while Alixana, will discover a secret that will change her life and also the whole Sarantium.<br /><br />I have to say that it is absolutely incredible how Kay manages through so many characters to make such a strong story and, even though this book has even less fantasy elements than the previous book (almost none I could say), makes you also to love them all.<br />Of course, I did wanted a little more fantasy in the mix but, as I said in my review in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1661412967" rel="nofollow noopener"> Sailing to Sarantium</a>
</i>, Kay’s writing, the plot, the characters and the twists all make a wonderful and unique story.<br /><br />Definitely one of his best works, by far!<br /><br /><br />Ελληνική κριτική:<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Το δεύτερο βιβλίο είναι ένα ακόμα εξαιρετικό παράδειγμα του ταλέντου του Kay καθώς έχει βάλει πολύ περισσότερους χαρακτήρες από το προηγούμενο κάνοντας μια πολύπλοκη ιστορία γεμάτη με ίντριγκες, δολοπλοκίες, προδοσίες αλλά και υπέροχες ανατροπές.<br /><br />Η ιστορία συνεχίζει με τον Rustem, έναν γιατρό από την Bassania, που καλείται από τον King of Kings να του σώσει την ζωή του αλλά και να πάρει μια αποστολή στο Sarantium που μπορεί να τον φέρει σε κίνδυνο να χάσει την δική του. Όμως στην πορεία θα βρεθεί σε πολλά διλήμματα καθώς θα πρέπει να αντιμετωπίσει όχι μόνο τις δολοπλοκίες του Sarantium αλλά και τα ίδια του τα πιστεύω.<br />Στην διάρκεια, ο Crispin, έχοντας αρχίσει το όνειρο του για την διακόσμηση του ναού του Jad θα βρεθεί σε δύσκολες καταστάσεις, καθώς θα πρέπει να επιλέξει ανάμεσα σε φίλους και εχθρούς, έρωτα και προδοσία ενώ η Alixana θα ανακαλύψει ένα μυστικό που θα αλλάξει για πάντα την ζωή της αλλά και όλου του Sarantium.<br /><br />Πρέπει να πω ότι είναι εκπληκτικό το πώς καταφέρνει ο Kay μέσα από τόσους πολλούς χαρακτήρες να βγάλει μια τόσο δυνατή ιστορία και, παρόλο που σε αυτό το βιβλίο έχει βάλει ακόμα λιγότερα φανταστικά στοιχεία από το προηγούμενο (σχεδόν καθόλου θα έλεγα), καταφέρνει επίσης να σε κάνει, πραγματικά, να τους αγαπήσεις όλους.<br />Φυσικά θα ήθελα να είχε λίγο παραπάνω φαντασία αλλά, όπως είπα και παραπάνω, η γραφή του Kay, η πλοκή, οι χαρακτήρες και οι ανατροπές κάνουν μια απίστευτη αλλά και μοναδική ιστορία.<br /><br />Σίγουρα ένα από τα καλύτερα έργα του Kay, μακράν! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

    <a title="Ross" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13012333-ross"><img alt="Ross" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523208735p2/13012333.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/601760110?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ross" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ross" href="/user/show/13012333-ross">Ross</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451463548" href="/book/show/8599967-lord-of-emperors">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer601760110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16647027419682911368">Where to begin with this one??? I guess I should tell you that I was trying not to cry (without success) after reading the first 30 pages. I did in fact cry a little bit, which is extremely rare for me to do while reading. I shed some tears and then walked outside to get some fresh air. Suddenly people were asking me why my eyes were so bloodshot? I'd been discovered!!! Of course in defense of my manhood I had to lie and tell them that I was just tired and had been yawning a lot. I thought that </span>
  <span id="freeText16647027419682911368" style="display:none">Where to begin with this one??? I guess I should tell you that I was trying not to cry (without success) after reading the first 30 pages. I did in fact cry a little bit, which is extremely rare for me to do while reading. I shed some tears and then walked outside to get some fresh air. Suddenly people were asking me why my eyes were so bloodshot? I'd been discovered!!! Of course in defense of my manhood I had to lie and tell them that I was just tired and had been yawning a lot. I thought that tidbit was worth mentioning, but now let me get to my thoughts on this amazing book.<br /><br />Book two of "The Sarantine Mosaic" picks up right where Book one left us. Crispin the Mosaicist is now fully involved with all of the scheming involved in the Sarantine political world. We are introduced to several new characters including a Physician from the mysterious east and a King who would intimidate Alexander the Great. I was nervous when Kay introduced yet another character because I didn't want the ending to be rushed! I should never have doubted because Kay pulls it off wonderfully, and when the story came to an end, I felt satisfied and rewarded. The story was original and compelling just like book one, and if you read book's one and two back to back, then everything makes sense. I never got lost, or confused when I was reading. I was comfortable in Sarantium, peering in on the lives of the characters. Some fantasy books can get complicated in regards to names, places, events, etc. but with Kay it all flowed very well.<br /><br />I could go on and on about Kay's prose, but you really should just read for yourself. My only complaint about this book was that it had to end! Kay could have easily written another novel set in the world of Sarantium and made this a trilogy. It was sad to say goodbye to the characters, but it was also refreshing to have a good old fashioned ending, not some cliffhanger or tease for a spin off. I already purchased another book written by Mr. Kay, and I suggest you do the same if your a fan of Fantasy. Once again, I'm glad I gave this a chance. Some writers pen entertaining stories and others write beautiful ones, Kay's Sarantium Duology is of the latter. Go check him out!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16647027419682911368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2116544706" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2116544706" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45400990-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573825853p2/45400990.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2116544706?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/45400990-laura">Laura</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2116544706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12077874584477841354">Buddy read with the Kay Squad from FBR <br/><br/>Duology review: 5 brilliant stars!!<br/><br/>My favourite Kay books so far. This duology is, simply put, a masterpiece!!<br/><br/>A wonderful homage to art, to women, to beauty and to love!<br/><br/>Kay's writing is elegant, almost poetic at times and rich beyond measure at others, matching the greatness and opulence of Sarantium. He will take you on a journey through which you will just have to pay attention to the road, having no clue whatsoever as to the destination. But it is all wo</span>
  <span id="freeText12077874584477841354" style="display:none">Buddy read with the Kay Squad from FBR <br><br>Duology review: 5 brilliant stars!!<br><br>My favourite Kay books so far. This duology is, simply put, a masterpiece!!<br><br>A wonderful homage to art, to women, to beauty and to love!<br><br>Kay's writing is elegant, almost poetic at times and rich beyond measure at others, matching the greatness and opulence of Sarantium. He will take you on a journey through which you will just have to pay attention to the road, having no clue whatsoever as to the destination. But it is all worth it!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1559386162i/27601582.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><i>Full Painting of The Sarantine Mosaic used in French Canadian editions</i><br><br>In itself, the story is simple. Crispin, a brilliant mosaicist, <i>still grieving for the family he lost to the plague, who lives only for his arcane craft and cares little for ambition, less for money, and for intrigue not at all,</i> must answer an imperial summons to Sarantium, to work on the greatest art work ever imagined.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1559386162i/27601583._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>geofftaylor-artist.com</i><br><br><i>In this world still half-wild and tangled with magic, no journey is simple. Bearing with him a Queen’s seductive promise, Crispin sets out for the fabled city from which none return unaltered, guarded only by his wits and a bird soul talisman from an alchemist’s treasury.</i><br><br>The first half of the first book describes his journey, during which Crispin risks his life, gets into trouble, and wins the devotion of a clever former slave girl, the loyalty of a hired hand of few words and sound deeds, and the friendship and respect of a rough and foul-mouthed commander.<br>The second part of the book deals with Crispin’s introduction to the Sarantium court and introduces us to the ruthless game for power, in which our unsuspecting mosaicist has already unwittingly become an important player.<br><br>And this is what the second book portrays: the political intrigue in Sarantium, a city of power, the seat of the Emperor of the East, a city and world based on Byzantium in the 5th or 6th centuries.<br><br>With masterful skill Kay builds for us here a wondrous mosaic of chariot racers, soldiers, politicians, dancers, actors, prostitutes, doctors and artisans. with Crispin, our main protagonist, smack down in the middle of it all.<br>He is now working on the dome of the biggest house of worship ever created; the crowning jewel of the rain of Emperor Valentine II, his legacy to the world.<br><br>The Emperor himself, along his exquisitely beautiful wife Aliana are dancing on a knife's edge in a court full of dangerous albeit subtle intrigue where the tiniest nuance may mean the difference between life and death. Two bold visionaries with plans that may hold dire consequences for entire kingdoms, their fate becomes tightly entwined with that of Crispin and by extension, with that of Queen Gisel, Crispin's own monarch exiled in Sarantium herself. <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1559387335i/27601724._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Keith Birdsong Lord of Emperors: Book Two of the Sarantine Mosaic Paperback Novel Cover Painting Original Art</i><br><br>Art and beauty are foremost present in these books.<br>From the description of the masterful mosaics to that of the fabled Sarantium, from the view of a forest in sunlight to the sight of a mythic zubir standing in a clearing at dawn, above a little bird lying on its side on the grass, Kay gives us art and beauty.<br><br>Then we have the women. Clever and strong women! Women that take a man’s breath away with their beauty then make him fear for his life the next minute with just their wits for a weapon. Kay honours women not only by setting them in high places in the great power game, but also by giving them courage, strength and resilience, even if they are mere slave girls. He even honours them through memory, a lost love, a lost wife, a lost girl, whose memory will never leave the man to whom they once belonged.<br><br>And finally the love! <br>Love not as sex but as feeling. Crispin’s still burning love for his lost wife. The love of a man for a few captured souls for whose freedom he is ready to lay down his life. The love of emperor Valerius for his beloved Alixana, which is so deceptively simple described that one cannot help but feel its intensity:<br><br><i>The Emperor reads no mystic certainties of any kind in the late-night flames, sitting at the woman’s feet, one hand touching her instep and the jewelled slipper. He says, “Never leave me.”<br>“Wherever would I go?” she murmurs after a moment, trying to keep the tone light and just failing.<br>He looks up. “Never leave me,” he says again, the grey eyes on hers this time.<br>He can do this to her, take breath from chest and throat. A constriction of great need. After all these years.<br>“Not in life,” she replies.</i><br><br>And last, but not least, the love of art. Love brought to us by a simple craftsman, willing to risk being maimed or blinded to follow his dream, to create the greatest artwork ever imagined, a masterpiece to be remembered for ages to come.<br><br>How far would you go to follow your dream? How far will Crispin go? You're gonna have to read this to find out but I guarantee ... it's worth it!! With his exquisite prose and elegant style, Kay will make you realize the beauty of living, the fleetingness and fragility of life! He will show you how every little moment is precious, a gift to be treasured and enjoyed!<br><br>I highly recommend this to all those who love beauty, strength and life! Smooth sailing on the winds of fate!!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1559387952i/27601740._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Cover of Sailing to Sarantium (Croatian hardback)</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="12077874584477841354" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_422817632" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/422817632" />
    <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper"><img alt="Kat  Hooper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533675060p2/2177891.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/422817632?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kat  Hooper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kat  Hooper" href="/user/show/2177891-kat-hooper">Kat  Hooper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2177891-kat-hooper?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer422817632" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6134992144492422277"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/lord-of-emperors/'>http://www.fantasyliterature.com/revi...</a><br/><br/>Lord of Emperors is the second (and final) novel in Guy Gavriel Kay’s THE SARANTINE MOSAIC duology. The story, set in a pseudo-Byzantine Empire, mostly centers on Crispin, a mosaicist from a neighboring kingdom who’s been commissioned to decorate the ceiling of a new chapel the emperor is building. Against his wishes, Crispin has been drawn into the Sarantine court’s political intrigue. In this second installment, the political turmoil finally comes to a h</span>
  <span id="freeText6134992144492422277" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com/reviews/lord-of-emperors/">http://www.fantasyliterature.com/revi...</a><br /><br />Lord of Emperors is the second (and final) novel in Guy Gavriel Kay’s THE SARANTINE MOSAIC duology. The story, set in a pseudo-Byzantine Empire, mostly centers on Crispin, a mosaicist from a neighboring kingdom who’s been commissioned to decorate the ceiling of a new chapel the emperor is building. Against his wishes, Crispin has been drawn into the Sarantine court’s political intrigue. In this second installment, the political turmoil finally comes to a head and Crispin’s life is, once again, drastically altered by events he can’t control. Not only are his and his friends’ lives in danger, but the changing political climate has major consequences for his art.<br /><br />While reading Sailing to Sarantium, the first book in the THE SARANTINE MOSAIC, I had a hard time believing in the characters and the drama — I thought the plot lacked the world-shaking significance that the characters seemed to be overwhelmed by at every moment. I felt manipulated — like Kay was showing me murder, lust, adultery, shocking brutality, witty repartee, and titillating suggestions to make me feel like there was more going on than there really was. While I liked Kay’s characters, it felt like a big soap opera to me and I was impatient with the story.<br /><br />The first part of Lord of Emperors is more of the slow drama and introspection that occurred in Sailing to Sarantium — every character analyzing what everyone else says, scrutinizing each gesture, contemplating every look, even reporting how they would think about this word or that gesture when they looked back on it sometime in the future. We’re reminded over and over how subtle and dangerous everybody is:<br /><br /><i>The room seemed laden and layered with intricacies of past and present and what was to come. Nuances coiling and spreading like incense, subtle and insistent.</i><br /><br />There are several sweet and touching scenes, but most of Lord of Emperors is more of the melodrama of Sailing to Sarantium. Finally, about 2/3 of the way through, there is a major upheaval followed by a slow unwinding of its tragic consequences. There are some real heartbreaking scenes in the last third of the novel, and the story ends on a beautifully bittersweet note. It just takes a really long time to get there.<br />Guy Gavriel Kay’s strength is making his characters come alive. Thus, when the big events finally occur, they really are painful and tragic and we worry about these people’s futures. I cared about Crispin, his queen, the charioteers, and the cook and his apprentice. However, I didn’t feel the need to be privy to every thought they had along the way — how many times do I need to be told that Crispin is thinking that only two women in the world wear a particular perfume? THE SARANTINE MOSAIC should have been trimmed down to just one book — I would have enjoyed it a lot more.<br /><br />I also think I would have felt more appreciation for THE SARANTINE MOSAIC if I had read it earlier in my acquaintance with Guy Gavriel Kay’s work. His world and characters are so full of life, there’s so much drama and passion, and I admire the character development. At this point in my reading history, however, I can’t help but notice that Kay’s intrusive style, which I’ve always thought of as almost over-the-top, never changes. Now that I’ve read ten of his novels, what I once admired — the type of story, the deep characterization, that particular distinctive prose — starts to become tiresome. If you’re new to Kay, or if you can’t get enough of his style, you’ll have a better experience with THE SARANTINE MOSAIC than I did.<br /><br />Again I listened to Audible Frontier’s audio production which was narrated by Berny Clark. Dialogue is his strength — I thought it was perfect. His narration is a little too slow (I had to speed him up) and I think some listeners will think it’s also a little bland, but I liked how his reading didn’t elevate Kay’s drama even further.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6134992144492422277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8504330683421494175">I'll cut to the chase, as I did with this sequel. I really, really wanted to be captivated with the elegant words and the characters, but I had to give up halfway and skim through. The truth is that personally, I believe that this book exceeds itself in length and suspense, and that the fact that most of the major, powerful female characters are intrigued by the protagonist is kind of incredible, in the literal sense. If the story had wrapped itself better and been concise, I'd loved it. By Jad,</span>
  <span id="freeText8504330683421494175" style="display:none">I'll cut to the chase, as I did with this sequel. I really, really wanted to be captivated with the elegant words and the characters, but I had to give up halfway and skim through. The truth is that personally, I believe that this book exceeds itself in length and suspense, and that the fact that most of the major, powerful female characters are intrigued by the protagonist is kind of incredible, in the literal sense. If the story had wrapped itself better and been concise, I'd loved it. By Jad, it's a shame. </span>

<div id="review_805275893" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/805275893" />
    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7177008-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388366904p2/7177008.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/805275893?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/7177008-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7177008-jane?shelf=historical-fantasy">historical-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer805275893" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2831935126941670183">An amazing book! I was really surprised and pleased that Part 2 of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19381540.The_Sarantine_Mosaic__Sailing_to_Sarantium_and_Lord_of_Emperors_' title='The Sarantine Mosaic (Sailing to Sarantium and Lord of Emperors) by Guy Gavriel Kay' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Sarantine Mosaic</a> was even better than the first Part! It was easy to figure out it was Kay's conception of a fantasy Byzantium. I'm so glad that not only did he concentrate on Crispin, the Rhodian [Roman] mosaicist but that there were fascinating subplots involving characters from the previous novel. I liked his introduction of a sympathetic Bassanid [Persian] physician, Rustem. Rustem had been sent as a spy by the King of Ki</span>
  <span id="freeText2831935126941670183" style="display:none">An amazing book! I was really surprised and pleased that Part 2 of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19381540.The_Sarantine_Mosaic__Sailing_to_Sarantium_and_Lord_of_Emperors_" title="The Sarantine Mosaic (Sailing to Sarantium and Lord of Emperors) by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sarantine Mosaic</a> was even better than the first Part! It was easy to figure out it was Kay's conception of a fantasy Byzantium. I'm so glad that not only did he concentrate on Crispin, the Rhodian [Roman] mosaicist but that there were fascinating subplots involving characters from the previous novel. I liked his introduction of a sympathetic Bassanid [Persian] physician, Rustem. Rustem had been sent as a spy by the King of Kings; being a actual practicing physician is an excellent cover story. His compassion and humanity as a doctor win out over espionage. The story moved quickly. I found myself actually caring about the characters and their fates. <br /><br />The author did an excellent job of describing the physical Sarantium [Byzantium] and its customs. He must have done much research to get the details so accurate. I could really visualize the city. He did play around with historical events. As the book was a historical fantasy, I didn't expect it to be historically accurate. I found myself in the big chariot race, yelling at the characters. That, and also when Emperor Valerius II [Justinian] was returning to the palace through the tunnel were nerve-wracking. I was glad to see Crispin's concept for his mosaic and follow the progress of his artistry. I found a recurring theme: several characters wanted to leave something of themselves that people would remember them, e.g., Emperor Valerius II with his beautiful church; Crispin, signing his mosaics; the charioteers like Scortius and Taras [both of the Blues] who would be remembered for centuries. The master chef would be remembered through his talented sous-chef, Kyros, his 'legacy'. Cleander, the wayward son of the Senator, matured and left his adolescence behind, the day of the big chariot race, writing a history years later: so people could remember. Schoolboys would quote from his text... <br /><br />I liked that Kay recapped enough from the first part that you could read this novel as a stand-alone. I do think it works best in order, though. The ending was most satisfactory all around. I feel Kay tied up everything neatly. Most highly recommended! </span>
  <a data-text-id="2831935126941670183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12357716213679430845" style="display:none">What a befitting sequel to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104097.Sailing_to_Sarantium__The_Sarantine_Mosaic___1_" title="Sailing to Sarantium (The Sarantine Mosaic, #1) by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Sailing to Sarantium</a>!   <span id="freeText471147335294612894" style="display:none">Probably only a 4.5, but there are so few really good reads out there, that I'm giving Kay extra credit. ;-) <br /><br />He completed a broad, subtle effort in just two books. <div id="review_255860189" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/255860189" />
    <a title="Puddin Pointy-Toes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3148911-puddin-pointy-toes"><img alt="Puddin Pointy-Toes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1263750297p2/3148911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/255860189?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Puddin Pointy-Toes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Puddin Pointy-Toes" href="/user/show/3148911-puddin-pointy-toes">Puddin Pointy-Toes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780451463548" href="/book/show/8599967-lord-of-emperors">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer255860189" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2717039168390213350">Like with <i>Under Heaven</i>, I found the ending to a let-down. Kay seems to be desperate to wraps up every loose end, to close the book on the entire life of nearly every character you've come to care about, and sometimes the epilogue he writes for these characters is inadequte to their due. It's a shame, because I like a little mystery when a good book comes to a close: I enjoy being left wanting more, but Kay doesn't want to give me more, and is adverse to leaving the reader with room for speculati</span>
  <span id="freeText2717039168390213350" style="display:none">Like with <i>Under Heaven</i>, I found the ending to a let-down. Kay seems to be desperate to wraps up every loose end, to close the book on the entire life of nearly every character you've come to care about, and sometimes the epilogue he writes for these characters is inadequte to their due. It's a shame, because I like a little mystery when a good book comes to a close: I enjoy being left wanting more, but Kay doesn't want to give me more, and is adverse to leaving the reader with room for speculation, it seems.<br /><br />This disappoints me, but despite this it's brilliantly written. I may not read another of his books, however---or at least not anytime soon. I've had enough disappointment for now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2717039168390213350" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer17700222518812736565">A long sleepy story occasionally punctuated with interesting events. I'm not sure what Guy Gavriel Kay was trying to capture when writing The Sarantine Mosaic, unfortunately it wasn't my engrossed attention. Entirely too much narrative throughout both volumes with an ending that collapsed upon itself with a silent thud. The story itself isn't without merit. I think there could have been so much done with this. Perhaps there was just too much and too many characters put into too few pages. Each c</span>
  <span id="freeText17700222518812736565" style="display:none">A long sleepy story occasionally punctuated with interesting events. I'm not sure what Guy Gavriel Kay was trying to capture when writing The Sarantine Mosaic, unfortunately it wasn't my engrossed attention. Entirely too much narrative throughout both volumes with an ending that collapsed upon itself with a silent thud. The story itself isn't without merit. I think there could have been so much done with this. Perhaps there was just too much and too many characters put into too few pages. Each character is beautifully developed at the expense of the story arc. I'm sorry this wasn't up with his other works. It had such potential.</span>

<div id="review_1210811085" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1210811085" />
    <a title="Lark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9658616-lark"><img alt="Lark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436313269p2/9658616.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1210811085?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lark" href="/user/show/9658616-lark">Lark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9658616-lark?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9658616-lark?shelf=empire">empire</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1210811085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9645585934882673524">The second book of the Sarantine Mosaic. We follow the potential war between cities, the chariot races underlying the tension between Blues and Greens, and of course the characters that are trying to stay afloat and survive in their daily lives.<br/><br/>I almost hate that it ended so well because it makes me really want to give 3 stars when I feel like it only deserved 1 or maybe 2 around the middle of the book. I seriously almost dnf around page 200 (about 50%) of the book because it got so boring and a</span>
  <span id="freeText9645585934882673524" style="display:none">The second book of the Sarantine Mosaic. We follow the potential war between cities, the chariot races underlying the tension between Blues and Greens, and of course the characters that are trying to stay afloat and survive in their daily lives.<br /><br />I almost hate that it ended so well because it makes me really want to give 3 stars when I feel like it only deserved 1 or maybe 2 around the middle of the book. I seriously almost dnf around page 200 (about 50%) of the book because it got so boring and annoying.<br /><br />Again, the only redeeming factors were the court scenes, the chariot races, and the ending. How many ways can I praise the way GGK writes the court scenes? Court was brilliant as usual, with people vying for power and showing how hidden knowledge is power. Tension in every word.<br />Like the first book, the chariot races were full of passion and energy and heart-stopping moments. Brilliant. And I ended up loving all of the chariot characters for their passion.<br />And the ending. THE ENDING. Just wow. What a strong finish. The imagery of <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ three women vying for the throne  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> is too great.<br />However, I was not convinced that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Alixana would actually end up with Crispin. Seem like too much of a neat and tidy way to wrap up characters's endings  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />But let's talk about characters. What can I say? I just don't really like Cripin. He's such a dull (and fairly unlikable) main character. But whereas I hated/was ambivalent toward every single character in the first book, there are quite a number of characters I like here. Of course Scortius, First of the Blues. He's brilliantly written. Shirin of the Greens and how she wears her perfume. Strumosus the fussy chef and Kyros (that medic scene was heart wrenching). Alixana and Valerius are just too snarky and adorable together. But Crispin.... yeah. No.<br /><br />Another little thing: there was no resolution with the birds. Kinda gimmicky because they were hardly used in this second book. The entire "magic" of these series was a underused.<br /><br />But as much as I complain about the problems, I just have to return to praise GGK. The prose... How does GGK write so beautifully? It is not flowery, it is not whimsical, or wispy around the edges. It <i>writhes</i> with emotion. It paints pictures in the mind.<br /><br />If I ever need to find beauty in words, I know which author to read.<br /><br />Three stars. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9645585934882673524" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1453551678700427855" style="display:none">Guy Gavriel Kay is amazing. Seriously, amazing. He never fails to move me. <br /><br />After finishing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104097.Sailing_to_Sarantium__The_Sarantine_Mosaic___1_" title="Sailing to Sarantium (The Sarantine Mosaic, #1) by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Sailing to Sarantium</a>, I said in my review that I wouldn't know how I felt about The Sarantine Mosaic until I finished <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104091.Lord_of_Emperors__The_Sarantine_Mosaic___2_" title="Lord of Emperors (The Sarantine Mosaic, #2) by Guy Gavriel Kay" rel="nofollow noopener">Lord of Emperors</a>. Having now finished it (obviously, hence this review), I have to say... Wow. This book was just.. Amazing. From beginning to end, I loved it. There were so many heart wrenching moments, beautifully written, that just drilled into the core of me and left me speechless. I know, that sounds extreme. It's just a book after all.. But it was just that good for me. Without a doubt.<br /><br />The story was bittersweet.. Love and loss, war and change.. But hope too. Always a little hope for a better tomorrow. The last section of the epilogue literally gave me chills. If I had a squishier heart, I probably would have cried. But alas, I do not, so alas, I did not. But still.. Loved it.<br /><br />This duology might just have moved to the top of my Kay rankings. I don't know though.. Seems like every time I read a new Kay it becomes my new favorite by him. He just plain rocks.. Enough said. </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2020526291" />
    <a title="T.L." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31467111-t-l"><img alt="T.L." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1568900435p2/31467111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2020526291?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="T.L." class="user" itemprop="url" name="T.L." href="/user/show/31467111-t-l">T.L.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/9746672-lord-of-emperors">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2020526291" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17659123149121224880">I've decided there is nothing quite like reading Guy Gavriel Kay. There's nothing quite like the feeling I've had after finishing the last words of two of his intricate stories: sorrow, sure; a bit of residual anger over the unfairness of things, this lingers; peace, overwhelmingly so.<br/><br/>Experiencing the way he pulls the threads of another magnificent story to their conclusion is truly remarkable. Somehow he is both able to surprise me and able to make me say, "Of course." and "How could it be anyt</span>
  <span id="freeText17659123149121224880" style="display:none">I've decided there is nothing quite like reading Guy Gavriel Kay. There's nothing quite like the feeling I've had after finishing the last words of two of his intricate stories: sorrow, sure; a bit of residual anger over the unfairness of things, this lingers; peace, overwhelmingly so.<br /><br />Experiencing the way he pulls the threads of another magnificent story to their conclusion is truly remarkable. Somehow he is both able to surprise me and able to make me say, "Of course." and "How could it be anything but?". I have never felt this...whatever it is...to this degree with another author. His stories are <i>right</i> and just as they ought to be--even when they are heartbreaking, or perhaps especially <i>because</i> they are heartbreaking.<br /><br />As I've said before, Kay is a master of revealing his story, and while this unwrapping is so carefully done, while this reverse origami is so precise and decidedly calculated, it still feels natural. I don't feel manipulated, even though I am aware (in the end, at least) that there has not been a single moment of my reading experience that the author did not control. As a reader, I am delighted, transported, and entranced. As a writer, I must look on in wonder--and then I must attempt to learn.<br /><br />P.S. I do hope Pertennius came to an unpleasant end.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17659123149121224880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/307652449" />
    <a title="Marit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1419866-marit"><img alt="Marit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598742551p2/1419866.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/307652449?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marit" href="/user/show/1419866-marit">Marit</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1419866-marit?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi-speculative-fiction">fantasy-sci-fi-speculative-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer307652449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6897089836407716863">Guy Gavriel Kay certainly kept me turning the pages on this wrap-up to his Sarantine Mosaic duo. We're introduced to a few more wonderful characters like the charming, indomitable, and convoluted young-old soul, Scortius, the champion charioteer of the Blues. And he deepens and revisits old characters thoroughly as well. Though the plot thickens and the entire last third of the book is an intricate, hair-pin turn denouement of sorts, this second book is as much about character development as the</span>
  <span id="freeText6897089836407716863" style="display:none">Guy Gavriel Kay certainly kept me turning the pages on this wrap-up to his Sarantine Mosaic duo. We're introduced to a few more wonderful characters like the charming, indomitable, and convoluted young-old soul, Scortius, the champion charioteer of the Blues. And he deepens and revisits old characters thoroughly as well. Though the plot thickens and the entire last third of the book is an intricate, hair-pin turn denouement of sorts, this second book is as much about character development as the first. There are some plot incidents that have nothing to do with the overarching beat of the story (like Scortius' love affairs(s)) but add to the rich tapestry of life that Kay has created. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6897089836407716863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer407908822" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13094912254293537415">Tremendous follow up to Kay's SAILING TO SARANTIUM. This is historical/fantasy fiction at its finest. It is not, however, "true" fantasy, though I say that with some trepidation. In LORD OF EMPERORS, Kay documents the Byzantine Empire and its changes, sometimes quoting directly from the history books and putting those quotes in the mouths of his fictionalized-but-real historical figures. Best thing I can say about this novel, having read the two part series, is that when I finished the book, I c</span>
  <span id="freeText13094912254293537415" style="display:none">Tremendous follow up to Kay's SAILING TO SARANTIUM. This is historical/fantasy fiction at its finest. It is not, however, "true" fantasy, though I say that with some trepidation. In LORD OF EMPERORS, Kay documents the Byzantine Empire and its changes, sometimes quoting directly from the history books and putting those quotes in the mouths of his fictionalized-but-real historical figures. Best thing I can say about this novel, having read the two part series, is that when I finished the book, I closed it the way we do when we read something special, with regret and a sigh that we would not be returning to that world. Highly recommended.</span>
